Larry Giddings, an anti authoritarian political prisoner was released on
parole in August, 2004. He served more than 20 years for his
anti-colonialist, anti-imperialist, anti-authoritarian views. The charges
against him were adding escape, assault on police officer, conspiracy to
effect escape and bank expropriations. He is currently staying in a half way
house and looking for a job. To be able to continue on parole, i.e. to
remain free he must find himself a job and place to live. Both take money.

Larry participated in the �Arts and Writings Against the Death Penalty�
project which raised funds for Mumia Abu Jamal�s legal defense.  Political
prisoners from around the US and from other countries would donate their
writings, poetry, paintings, drawings, ceramics, etc to be exhibited at art
galleries, universities, churches etc throughout the US and other countries.
Funds raised from those that were sold were sent to Mumia�s legal defense.
Larry contributed a number of wonderful paintings. We found out just what a
creative and generous person he is. And now its our turn to be generous.

Please help Larry remain free and get on his feet by sending your tax
deductible donation (check or money order made out to IFCO/Prolibertad, with
Larry Giddings written in the memo section) to address below. Thank you

IFCO/Prolibertad
402 West 145th Street
New York, N.Y. 10031
_______________________________________________________________________________

Many of you may have known George personally; for others this may be the
first you have heard of this IRA-gunrunning, socialist legend.

George was one of those Irish immigrants that refused to fade "from Green to
White" as they crossed the Atlantic Ocean.  Ever the good socialist, George
consistently took courageous stands against racism and US Imperialism.  He
never shied away from supporting the liberation struggles of oppressed
peoples here in the US simply because it upset the more conservative
elements in the Irish community.  In fact, he seemed to relish in upsetting
these elements.  I met George through the work of building an anti-racist,
anti-imperialist pole within the Irish community in New York.  Too often
racism went unchallenged within the community so long as someone had the
correct "Brit's out" line relating to the north of Ireland.  George was an
inspiration to myself and many others in challenging that attitude and
helping to build support within the Irish community for Cuba, Mumia
Abu-Jamal, and for Puerto Rican independence.   We have truly lost an elder
in the fight for social justice, self-determination and socialism.  I hope
you will take this moment to remember George as he would want to be
remembered:  by rededicating ourselves to the struggle...George Harrison
Presente!

George's Memorial will be held on October 20th at 6:30pm at Local 1199, 210
W.43rd St. (between 8th  & 9th Avenues, NYC).  The speakers confirmed at
this point are:  Bernadette Devlin McAliskey, David Dinkins, Jimmy Breslin,
and Dennis Rivera.

Unfortunately, George never wrote his autobiography.  A friend once told me
that when he tried to sit him down to do oral history, George would only
talk about Mumia and Fidel--never himself. Below are a couple of articles
about George and his illustrious career.  For more information on George's
life and politics see The American Connection by Jack Holland, chapter 5 "Of
Arms and the Man." (Amazon has copies for $3 bucks!)
_______________________________________________________________________________

Please see the following website to download the flyer for Farouk Abdel
Muhti's
Memorial, taking place November 13th at 6pm, 1199.

http://sp-usa.org/newflyer.pdf
_______________________________________________________________________________

Saturday Oct. 30th, 2004 at 7pm
Hostos Community College Auditorium
149th and Grand Concourse

On October 27, 1974, over 20,000 people gathered at Madison Square Garden,
demanding the freedom of our then political prisoners and the independence
of Puerto Rico. With giant pictures of our nationalist heroes and our
heroine, Lolita Lebron hanging from above, progressive and oppressed sectors
of the North American society and over ten UN delegations.  Event was
historical in countless other ways.

It was the first time in this country that 20,000 people came together to
demand the freedom for our Puerto Rican Nationalist  and freedom for our
nation.  It also proved beyond doubt the Puerto Rican community could be
mobilized and the power of good organization  can have in mobilizing it.
However the most impressive aspect of the activity was fact that the
majority of the people that filled the Garden  that day came from within our
communities and from workplaces as far as Gary, Indiana where had Puerto
Ricans had migrated to work in the steel industry.

This gathering  was the product of an intensive educational and
organizational effort  undertaken by the members of the  Puerto Rican
Socialist Party(PSP) �Seccional� or section of the party organized here in
the belly of the beast in conjunction with progressive sectors in this
country. The program included solidarity messages from representatives of
these sectors including Dr. Angela Davis
The effort would  establish and consolidate the political and organizational
presence of the PSP in our communities and in a number of workplaces
resulting  in perhaps the biggest independence and socialist  Puerto Rican
organization in this country. It continued with  a period of numerous
struggles in many fronts from housing rent strikes to union organizing.
Additionally   it would provide the basis and the motor for what was later
to be the massive Bicentennial with out Colonies march held in Washington DC
two years later in 1976.

On October 30th 2004 we will hold another gathering, this time to celebrate
the 30th anniversary of the �Acto en el Garden� as is sometimes refered to.
As in the 1974 activity Juan Mari Bras will be the main speaker. Juan Mari
will be joined by representatives of some of the progressive forces in this
country and will culminate with a cultural activity by Roy Brown.

Unfortunately not all  those who accompanied us thirty years ago will be at
Hostos but perhaps this coming together even if you were not there the first
time, can serve to recoup our energies, optimism and  will to continue the
many struggles as the Garden rally did thirty years ago. Remember to join us
on October 30th 2004, at 7:00 pm at Hostos Community College on 149th Street
and Grand Concourse in the Bronx.

parole in August, 2004. He served more than 20 years for his
anti-colonialist, anti-imperialist, anti-authoritarian views. The charges
against him were adding escape, assault on police officer, conspiracy to
effect escape and bank expropriations. He is currently staying in a half way
house and looking for a job. To be able to continue on parole, i.e. to
remain free he must find himself a job and place to live. Both take money.

Larry participated in the �Arts and Writings Against the Death Penalty�
project which raised funds for Mumia Abu Jamal�s legal defense.  Political
prisoners from around the US and from other countries would donate their
writings, poetry, paintings, drawings, ceramics, etc to be exhibited at art
galleries, universities, churches etc throughout the US and other countries.
Funds raised from those that were sold were sent to Mumia�s legal defense.
Larry contributed a number of wonderful paintings. We found out just what a

Save the Date: Farouk Memorial on November 13

A Memorial for
Farouk Abdel-Muhti, 1947-2004

Saturday, November 13, 2004, 6-9pm
at 1199 Auditorium, 310 W. 43rd St. in Manhattan

For information contact:
abufkheida@..., 201-951-6919 (Sharin Chiorazzo)
freefarouk@..., 212-674-9499 (David Wilson)

If you can help distribute flyers for this event please write to
freefarouk@....

The event is free, but donations are encouraged. Make checks payable to the
"Farouk Fund" and mail to CRFAM at PO Box 20587, Tompkins Square Station,
New York, NY 10009.

Dear Friends,

We invite you to join us on Saturday, November 13, in paying tribute to
our friend and comrade, Farouk Abdel-Muhti, who fought a long and noble
struggle for freedom and justice, then passed away tragically and suddenly
last July 21, only 100 days after his release from two years of
immigration detention.

A brief program at the memorial service will include video footage of
Farouk edited by Konrad Aderer, and speakers including radical attorney
Lynne Stewart. There will also be food and music.

We are preparing a special memorial booklet including photos, quotes and a
biography of Farouk which will be distributed at the event. Individuals
and organizations are encouraged to include their memorial messages in the
booklet. Camera-ready copy is greatly preferred. Please email your message
in the correct size (see below) to freefarouk@... no later than
October 27 (Preferred formats are MSWord or RTF for formatted text,
Illustrator eps for line art or tiff 200-300dpi for photos/scanned art.)
Camera-ready printouts can also be mailed or delivered to the Socialist
Party USA at 339 Lafayette St, New York, NY 10012 no later than October
27.

The booklet pages will be 5.5" wide x 8.5 high". Suggested donation rates
are as follows:

$80 for 1/2 page (5-1/4" wide x 4-1/8" high)
$50 for 1/4 page (2-5/8" wide x 4-1/8" high)
$30 for 1/8 page (2-5/8" wide x 2" high)
$15 for single line name listing

Please make your checks payable to the "Farouk Fund" and mail to CRFAM at
PO Box 20587, Tompkins Square Station, New York, NY 10009. Donations go
toward burial and headstone expenses and to help Farouk's son Tariq
through this difficult time.

Thank you for your support, and we look forward to seeing you at the
memorial event on Saturday, November 13.

Sincerely,

Sharin Chiorazzo (Farouk's fiancee)
Tariq Abdel-Muhti (Farouk's son)
David Wilson (Committee for the Release of Farouk Abdel-Muhti)
Greg Pason (Socialist Party USA and NJ Free Farouk Committee)
Jane Guskin (Coalition for the Human Rights of Immigrants)

Political Prisoners Dec 10th-12th!!

Larry Giddings, an anti authoritarian political prisoner was released on
parole in August, 2004. He served more than 20 years for his
anti-colonialist, anti-imperialist, anti-authoritarian views. The charges
against him were adding escape, assault on police officer, conspiracy to
effect escape and bank expropriations. He is currently staying in a half way
house and looking for a job. To be able to continue on parole, i.e. to
remain free he must find himself a job and place to live. Both take money.

Larry participated in the Arts and Writings Against the Death Penalty
project which raised funds for Mumia Abu Jamals legal defense.  Political
prisoners from around the US and from other countries would donate their
writings, poetry, paintings, drawings, ceramics, etc to be exhibited at art
galleries, universities, churches etc throughout the US and other countries.
Funds raised from those that were sold were sent to Mumias legal defense.
Larry contributed a number of wonderful paintings. We found out just what a
creative and generous person he is. And now its our turn to be generous.

Please help Larry remain free and get on his feet by sending your tax
deductible donation (check or money order made out to IFCO/Prolibertad, with
Larry Giddings written in the memo section) to address below. Thank you

IFCO/Prolibertad
402 West 145th Street
New York, N.Y. 10031
______________________________________________________________________________

Save the Date: Farouk Memorial on November 13

A Memorial for
Farouk Abdel-Muhti, 1947-2004

Saturday, November 13, 2004, 6-9pm
at 1199 Auditorium, 310 W. 43rd St. in Manhattan

For information contact:
abufkheida@..., 201-951-6919 (Sharin Chiorazzo)
freefarouk@..., 212-674-9499 (David Wilson)

If you can help distribute flyers for this event please write to
freefarouk@....

The event is free, but donations are encouraged. Make checks payable to the
"Farouk Fund" and mail to CRFAM at PO Box 20587, Tompkins Square Station,
New York, NY 10009.

Dear Friends,

We invite you to join us on Saturday, November 13, in paying tribute to
our friend and comrade, Farouk Abdel-Muhti, who fought a long and noble
struggle for freedom and justice, then passed away tragically and suddenly
last July 21, only 100 days after his release from two years of
immigration detention.

A brief program at the memorial service will include video footage of
Farouk edited by Konrad Aderer, and speakers including radical attorney
Lynne Stewart. There will also be food and music.

We are preparing a special memorial booklet including photos, quotes and a
biography of Farouk which will be distributed at the event. Individuals
and organizations are encouraged to include their memorial messages in the
booklet. Camera-ready copy is greatly preferred. Please email your message
in the correct size (see below) to freefarouk@... no later than
October 27 (Preferred formats are MSWord or RTF for formatted text,
Illustrator eps for line art or tiff 200-300dpi for photos/scanned art.)
Camera-ready printouts can also be mailed or delivered to the Socialist
Party USA at 339 Lafayette St, New York, NY 10012 no later than October
27.

The booklet pages will be 5.5" wide x 8.5 high". Suggested donation rates
are as follows:

$80 for 1/2 page (5-1/4" wide x 4-1/8" high)
$50 for 1/4 page (2-5/8" wide x 4-1/8" high)
$30 for 1/8 page (2-5/8" wide x 2" high)
$15 for single line name listing

Please make your checks payable to the "Farouk Fund" and mail to CRFAM at
PO Box 20587, Tompkins Square Station, New York, NY 10009. Donations go
toward burial and headstone expenses and to help Farouk's son Tariq
through this difficult time.

Thank you for your support, and we look forward to seeing you at the
memorial event on Saturday, November 13.

Sincerely,

Sharin Chiorazzo (Farouk's fiancee)
Tariq Abdel-Muhti (Farouk's son)
David Wilson (Committee for the Release of Farouk Abdel-Muhti)
Greg Pason (Socialist Party USA and NJ Free Farouk Committee)
Jane Guskin (Coalition for the Human Rights of Immigrants)

_______________________________________________________________________________

ProLibertad's weekly segment, Despierta Boricua, on WBAI's Wake Up Call with
Mario Murillo at 6:45am is airing another segment this Friday November 5th,
2004. This week�s segment will focus on the documentary the Boricua
Independent producer Felix Leo Campos is working on called, �MORALES FAMILY
HERITAGE: 50 Years of Boricuas En El Barrio!!�

While the focus of this story is Puerto Ricans (Boricuas), it represents the
greater number ethnic immigrant working classes that have made East Harlem a
home. The end product is a patch quilt community of co-existence among the
various successions of immigrants who settled in East Harlem, each with
their own name for their newly found ethnic community, i.e. El Barrio (The
Neighborhood).

Tune in Friday November 5th, 2004 at 6:45am WBAI 99.5fm, for those of you
not in NYC you can listen in via webstream at http://www.wbai.org

Jose Perez Gonzalez has been a victim of repression for the past several
months; he has been denied communication with families and legal support.
He has been pressured to give information on well-known Vieques leaders like
Ismael Guadalupe and Carlos Zenon, which he has refused to do.  He has been
kept in solitary confinement and has suffered other abuses at the hands of
prison officials.

He was incarcerated in a maximum security penitentiary in Atlanta, when he
should�ve been placed in a medium security prison.  Finally after months of
letter writing and support, Jose has been moved, please write to him at:

Jose Perez Gonzalez
#21519-069
Federal Prison Camp
P.O. Box 725
Edgefield, South Carolina 29824-0725
_______________________________________________________________________________

We wish to honor Safiya Bukhari by continuing the work she dedicated her 
life to, the struggle for recognition and release of all political 
prisoners/POWs.

We are producing the Safiya Bukhari Resource Guide as a tool for activists 
around the issue of political prisoners/POWs.

The Resource Guide will include many organizations working on the issue of 
political prisoners/POWs
both nationally and internationally.

It will be 8.5 x 5.5 inches. Full-page ads (4.5 x 7.5 in.) cost only $50,

News Bulletin: November 19, 2004

U.S. Parole Commission Weighs Continuing Supervision of Former Puerto Rican
Political Prisoners

On September 10, 2004, Edwin Cort�s, Elizam Escobar, Ricardo Jim�nez, Adolfo
Matos, Dylcia Pag�n, Alberto Rodr�guez, Ida Luz (Lucy) Rodr�guez,
Alejandrina Torres, and Carmen Valent�n marked the fifth anniversary of
their historic release from U.S. prisons. When president Clinton commuted
their disproportionate sentences, he deemed they had served two-thirds of
their sentences, making them eligible to serve the remaining third under the
supervision of the U.S. Parole Commission. Their supervision would last
until 2008 or 2009, unless the Parole Commission terminated the supervision
earlier. After the fifth anniversary, the Parole Commission may not continue
supervision unless it is determined, after a hearing, that such supervision
should not be terminated because there is a likelihood that the person will
engage in conduct violating any criminal law.

Today we learned that the Parole Commission has decided that it will not
accede to early termination of supervision, but that it will proceed to a
hearing, in spite of the fact that both of the supervising U.S. Probation
Officers recommended early termination; in spite of the fact that there is
no evidence that any of these men and women will engage in conduct violating
any criminal law; and in spite of the fact that we provided the Parole
Commission with extensive documentation of their successful integration into
civil society, including letters of support from their employers, as well as
elected officials and civic and religious leaders from Puerto Rico and the
U.S.

Hearings will be scheduled in San Juan and Chicago, to be presided over by
representatives of the Parole Commission. Their regulations provide for due
process, including that the right to be apprised of the evidence and the
right to confront and cross-examine adverse witnesses. We have already
formally requested that we be apprised of the evidence they intend to
introduce against each of the former political prisoners; and we have
indicated our intention to exercise the right to confront and cross-examine
any and all adverse witnesses.

Jan Susler

Attorney for Edwin Cort�s, Elizam Escobar, Ricardo Jim�nez, Adolfo Matos,
Dylcia Pag�n, Alberto Rodr�guez, Ida Luz (Lucy) Rodr�guez, Alejandrina
Torres, and Carmen Valent�n

People's Law Office
1180 N. Milwaukee
Chicago, IL 60622

773/235-0070 ext 118
______________________________________________________________________________

Despierta Boricua is a weekly fifteen minute Radio segment produced for WBAI 
99.5 FM Friday Morning Wake UP Call by The ProLibertad Freedom Campaign.  
Despierta Boricua airs at 6:45am, but is subject to re-scheduling depending 
on the Station�s needs.

Despierta Boricua is dedicated to providing the New York City listening 
audience with the latest news and updates on Puerto Rican politics and 
culture from U.S. based Puerto Rican communities and the island of Puerto 
Rico.

This week's segment features an interview with Hugo Delgado Marrti of La 
Union de Juventud Socialista (UJS-The Socialist Youth Union) of Puerto Rico. 
  Hugo will be talking with us about the recent student repression/police 
brutality in Puerto Rico and the controversay about the electoral ballots in 
Puerto Rico.

Tune in every week to Despierta Boricua, whether you receive a reminder or 
not, 6:45am WBAI 99.5fm or via webstream, for those of you outside of NYC, 
http://www.wbai.org

Oscar Lopez Rivera:  A Special Interview for Claridad
Eduardo Villanueva/Special to Claridad Newspaper Translated by Frank Velgara 
of ProLibertad

Today, October 15, 2004, Terre Haute, Indiana, in the U.S., I am visiting 
and conversing with Oscar Lopez Rivera, one of our political prisoners and a 
remnant of the days of  The Cold War.

He is 61 years old, small in build and has the stature of our traditional 
Puerto Rican peasant farmers.  He has a strong complexion with black eyes 
that glitter sharply and seem to be deeply piercing like the scalpel of an 
experienced surgeon.  He has a full mustache that he takes care of 
meticulously and a well combed pony tail in his hair that is barely 
noticeable in the scarce hair on his strikingly strong head.

His innate timidity falls away after breaking the ice and he embarks on a 
profound conversation which is logical, coherent and ingenious and seems to 
negate the effects of 23 years of incarceration.  During these 23 years, he 
served 12 years in Marion one of the cruelest and most inhumane prisons in 
the world.  He has been deprived of physical contact, and limited in his 
phone calls and visits in an attempt to break an indomitable spirit that is 
always with him and is reflected in each action in his rich and complex 
life.

He is a man of courage, self-taught, and disciplined.  He is willing to 
methodically organize the oppressed masses that languish in the Diaspora in 
the United States and who are suffocated by racism and the hatred that 
imperialism unleashes against all those who challenge its hegemony.

From the beginning you are impressed that throughout an extensive 
conversation that covers topics such as history, literature, sports, 
sociology and spiritual agnosticism, you seem to be with a Catholic 
Protestant, who is not interested in fishing for votes.  You don�t hear 
complaints, pessimism, feelinga of hatred or resentment coming from the 
suffering he has had to endure because of his love of the homeland and his 
revolutionary vocation.  Oscar never complains nor does he engage in long 
lamentations of all that he has had to suffer in prison because of his 
ideals.

Few people know in detail the physical isolation and sensory deprivation 
that he was subjected to at Marion.  Few also know what the jailers at 
Florence subjected Oscar to during his first 6 years of imprisonment there.  
For 58 days they woke him every 30 minutes with a bright light in order to 
deprive him of any rest and to prevent him from getting any uninterrupted 
sleep.  These are barbaric acts that only a spirit who is committed to the 
highest ideals of freedom can endure in the name of the love for his people.

Few people in the so-called free community are better informed than Oscar 
about the reality of his country.  He is familiar with all the ideological 
tendencies in the independence movement, their views and leaders, their 
weaknesses and strengths.   He recognizes the persistence, honesty, 
dedication and patriotism of leaders whose activity he has followed and 
carefully studied.   He condemns that fact that some political movements 
have become so bureaucratized and sometimes to the point of risking 
disappearing altogether.  But he recognizes that the diversity of the 
struggle and implementation of tactics and strategy have made it impossible 
for the empire to exterminate us despite all their repressive strategies and 
actions. They have been unleashed against us and are directed at violating 
our civil rights and our right to self-determination.

By listening respectfully and reflecting on his thinking and views, you can 
clearly come to understand why his jailers and U.S. intelligence agencies 
fear him to the point that they fabricated a supposed escape attempt in 
order to lengthen his sentence to 78 years in jail which is what it is 
today.

Oscar enjoys writing to the youth with whom he is in contact.  They  benefit 
from his teaching abilities and his self-taught knowledge.  Some professors 
invite Oscar to contact their students so that they can deepen their 
understanding of the political process in Puerto Rico, colonialism, the 
anti-imperialist struggle, the need for revolutionary changes and the 
dialectical process that allows a political prisoner to transform his 
incarceration and his separation from his loved ones into an experience of 
liberation and personal growth.

Oscar is very aware of his role as a patriotic role model although he 
doesn�t mention it or brag about it.  That is why in his writings he is 
careful not to promote farfetched or irresponsible ideas and to reject ideas 
that promote any personality cult. He rejects this because he considers it 
pure vanity and because he has learned from the negative impact that it has 
had in the ongoing revolutionary  struggle for social change as it pertains 
to other political leaders.

In thinking about his role with youth I would dare say  that Oscar believes 
as does Benedetti that: �the moral health and spiritual clarity of a young 
person will be the probable guarantee that the continuation of life will 
likely be clean and honest� (Benedetti, Memoria y Esperanza, P�g. 28).

I reflect upon the experience of being in such intense proximity to the soul 
of an incarcerated patriot and I think of the inexorable and 
incomprehensible absurdity that the freedom of the homeland demands such 
painful steps in its movement forward -- the existence of political 
prisoners.  I believe that today as always the people have a moral 
obligation to demand that they all return home.

The author is a lawyer, President of the Human Rights Committee and a Member 
of the Civil Rights Commission of Puerto Rico.

and conversing with Oscar Lopez Rivera, one of our political prisoners and a
remnant of the days of  The Cold War.

He is 61 years old, small in build and has the stature of our traditional
Puerto Rican peasant farmers.  He has a strong complexion with black eyes
that glitter sharply and seem to be deeply piercing like the scalpel of an
experienced surgeon.  He has a full mustache that he takes care of
meticulously and a well combed pony tail in his hair that is barely
noticeable in the scarce hair on his strikingly strong head.

His innate timidity falls away after breaking the ice and he embarks on a
profound conversation which is logical, coherent and ingenious and seems to
negate the effects of 23 years of incarceration.  During these 23 years, he
served 12 years in Marion one of the cruelest and most inhumane prisons in
the world.  He has been deprived of physical contact, and limited in his
phone calls and visits in an attempt to break an indomitable spirit that is
always with him and is reflected in each action in his rich and complex
life.

He is a man of courage, self-taught, and disciplined.  He is willing to
methodically organize the oppressed masses that languish in the Diaspora in
the United States and who are suffocated by racism and the hatred that
imperialism unleashes against all those who challenge its hegemony.

From the beginning you are impressed that throughout an extensive
conversation that covers topics such as history, literature, sports,
sociology and spiritual agnosticism, you seem to be with a Catholic
Protestant, who is not interested in fishing for votes.  You don�t hear
complaints, pessimism, feelinga of hatred or resentment coming from the
suffering he has had to endure because of his love of the homeland and his
revolutionary vocation.  Oscar never complains nor does he engage in long
lamentations of all that he has had to suffer in prison because of his
ideals.

Few people know in detail the physical isolation and sensory deprivation
that he was subjected to at Marion.  Few also know what the jailers at
Florence subjected Oscar to during his first 6 years of imprisonment there.
For 58 days they woke him every 30 minutes with a bright light in order to
deprive him of any rest and to prevent him from getting any uninterrupted
sleep.  These are barbaric acts that only a spirit who is committed to the
highest ideals of freedom can endure in the name of the love for his people.

Few people in the so-called free community are better informed than Oscar
about the reality of his country.  He is familiar with all the ideological
tendencies in the independence movement, their views and leaders, their
weaknesses and strengths.   He recognizes the persistence, honesty,
dedication and patriotism of leaders whose activity he has followed and
carefully studied.   He condemns that fact that some political movements
have become so bureaucratized and sometimes to the point of risking
disappearing altogether.  But he recognizes that the diversity of the
struggle and implementation of tactics and strategy have made it impossible
for the empire to exterminate us despite all their repressive strategies and
actions. They have been unleashed against us and are directed at violating
our civil rights and our right to self-determination.

By listening respectfully and reflecting on his thinking and views, you can
clearly come to understand why his jailers and U.S. intelligence agencies
fear him to the point that they fabricated a supposed escape attempt in
order to lengthen his sentence to 78 years in jail which is what it is
today.

Oscar enjoys writing to the youth with whom he is in contact.  They  benefit
from his teaching abilities and his self-taught knowledge.  Some professors
invite Oscar to contact their students so that they can deepen their
understanding of the political process in Puerto Rico, colonialism, the
anti-imperialist struggle, the need for revolutionary changes and the
dialectical process that allows a political prisoner to transform his
incarceration and his separation from his loved ones into an experience of
liberation and personal growth.

Oscar is very aware of his role as a patriotic role model although he
doesn�t mention it or brag about it.  That is why in his writings he is
careful not to promote farfetched or irresponsible ideas and to reject ideas
that promote any personality cult. He rejects this because he considers it
pure vanity and because he has learned from the negative impact that it has
had in the ongoing revolutionary  struggle for social change as it pertains
to other political leaders.

In thinking about his role with youth I would dare say  that Oscar believes
as does Benedetti that: �the moral health and spiritual clarity of a young
person will be the probable guarantee that the continuation of life will
likely be clean and honest� (Benedetti, Memoria y Esperanza, P�g. 28).

I reflect upon the experience of being in such intense proximity to the soul
of an incarcerated patriot and I think of the inexorable and
incomprehensible absurdity that the freedom of the homeland demands such
painful steps in its movement forward -- the existence of political
prisoners.  I believe that today as always the people have a moral
obligation to demand that they all return home.

The author is a lawyer, President of the Human Rights Committee and a Member
of the Civil Rights Commission of Puerto Rico.

Oscar Lopez Rivera:  A Special Interview for Claridad
Eduardo Villanueva/Special to Claridad Newspaper Translated by Frank Velgara
of ProLibertad

Today, October 15, 2004, Terre Haute, Indiana, in the U.S., I am visiting
and conversing with Oscar Lopez Rivera, one of our political prisoners and a
remnant of the days of  The Cold War.
